INTRODUCTORY NOTE ON SAMSKRIT GRAMMAR

  • Gita Mahatmyam.*‚ÄîThe greatness of the Gita as described in the Varaha‚ÄîPurana

  • Gita-Karadi-Nyasah.*‚ÄîThe Tantra way of ‘practising’ the Gita.

  • Gita‚ÄîDhyanam.*‚Äî‚ÄòThe Thought‚ÄîForm of the Gita for purposes of meditation

CHAP: I. Arjuna Vishada-Yoga.‚ÄîArjuna inspects the rival forces‚ÄîHe describes the evil consequent on the battles of kinsfolk

CHAP II. Sankhya-Yoga.‚ÄîArjuna refuses to fight‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa counsels Arjuna not to grieve over the mere destruction of transient bodies, because the inner Self can never be slain‚Äîor even because the bodies must die in any case‚Äî and so to perform the duty of the K·π£hattriya with untroubled mind‚Äîthereby gaining fame in this and happiness in the other world, incidentally‚Äîthough duty should be performed for its own sake and not for any ulterior reward, as it is performed by sages of steady mind‚ÄîArjuna asks for a more particular description of such‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa complies, laying stress on the attainment of wisdom and the absence of desire.

CHAP: III. Karma-Yoga.‚ÄîArjuna asks how these are compatible with such terrible action as that of slaughter-ShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa‚Äôs preliminary reconciliation of the two: ‚Äúperform the action that is duty, but without attachment‚Äù‚Äîa cursory allusion to the system of the world-wheel, which gives rise to the various Dharmas, duties‚ÄîArjuna enquires why men fail in duty‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa replies, cursorily as yet.

CHAP: IV…. Jnana-Vibhaga-Yoga‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa again lays stress on the necessity of Wisdom, right-thought, right-reason, and in this connection describes the many forms of Yoga‚ÄîHe adverts to His own previous incarnations, in reply to a question of Arjuna’s (and so incidentally strengthens the allusion, made before, to world-cycles, without an understanding of which true enlightenment is not possible).

CHAP V. Karma-Sannyasa-Yoga.‚ÄîArjuna repeats his question as to the inconsistency between ‘renunciation’ and ‘action’‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa answers (indirectly, by describing the happy condition of … those who renounce the fruits of action but continue to perform it as duty, knowing that all proceeds from the Supreme Self,

CHAP VI. Adhyatma-Yoga.‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa continues his description of the Yogi‚ÄîArjuna asks how the condition may be attained‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa

answers: ‚Äúby dispassion and perseverent practice.‚Äù‚ÄîArjuna asks: ‚Äúwhat are the consequences of failure in Yoga?‚Äú‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa replies: ‚Äúa better birth, greater opportunity and devotion to the Supreme Self, and final attainment of Moksha.‚Äù

CHAP: VII. Jnana-Yoga.‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa describes the natures of wisdom and unwisdom‚Äîthe Glories of the Self (the knowledge of which constitutes the wisdom whereby the possessor thereof performs his duty unflinchingly).

CHAP: VIII. Akshara-Brahma-Yoga.‚Äî(The subjectmatter of the previous chapter is continued.) Arjuna asks what is the nature of Brahman, of AdhyaÃÇtma, Adhibh√ªta, and Adhidaiva-ShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa describes these and also incidentally ora mentions how Brahman may be reached by the byliving as well as the departing JiÃÇva.

CHAP: IX.Raja-Vidya-Raja-Guhya-Yoga.‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa continues his statement of ‚Äúthe Royal Secret, the final wisdom,‚Äù the possession of which confers Immortality, viz, the recognition of and devotion to the Supreme Self‚Äîalso describes the results of devotion to minor personal Gods (IÃÇshvaras of systems &c.)

CHAP: X. Vibhuti - Yoga.‚ÄîThe Glories of the Supreme Self

CHAP: XI. Vishvarupa-darshana-Yoga.‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa compresses into one illuminating vision all the

results of lengthened explanations, and makes Arjuna see directly that he and all his fellow- beings are part of the life of IÃÇshvara-that they are what they are by His gift (in the personal aspect of the Supreme Self, manifesting as an Individual IÃÇshvara) that it is their duty to endeavour, feebly as they may, to repay this great debt by obeying His will, and that His will, in the present instance is the destruction of K·π£hattriyas.

** CHAP: XII. Bhakti-Yoga.**‚ÄîArjuna, perceiving the difference between the Unmanifested Eternal and the manifested IÃÇshvara, incidentally asks which is the better method, fixing the mind on the one or on the other-ShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa answers: ‚Äúthe former is the more difficult, and by that method too, JiÃÇvas have to pass to the stage and state of (a personal) IÃÇshvara.‚Äù (He thereby stimulates deeper enquiry, to which He leaves the discovery that the two systems are really not distinct, that the one system is to fix the mind on the Eternal, and, in subordination to that supreme fact, to work by the will of the IÃÇshvara to whom we belong.

CHAP: XIII. Kshetra-Kshetrajna-vibhaga-Yoga.‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa expounds the difference between Puru·π£ha and Prak·πõiti as part of the supreme science, the wisdom which raises the JiÃÇva above doubt and makes him steady in action as Arjuna should be.

** CHAP: XIV. Guna-traya-vibhaga-Yoga.**‚ÄîThe subject of the preceding chapter is continued and ion the three gu·πáas of Prakriti are dealt with.

CHAP : XV. Purushottama-Yoga.‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa carries on the subject of the 13th discourse, and deals specially with the Puru·π£ha, in the aspect of the Abstract Self as well as the aspect of a manifested IÃÇshvara.

** CHAP: XVI. Daivasura-Sampad-vibhaga-Yoga.**‚Äî ShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa goes on to describe, as another essential part of the highest science, and having, further, an immediate bearing on the issue before Arjuna, the dual nature of all the creatures of IÃÇshvara, divine and demoniac, good and evil‚ÄîHe tells how the former class, to which Arjuna belongs, necessarily perform their duty, in accordance with the ShaÃÇstras (and battle against the latter to prevent them from harming the innocent).

** CHAP: XVII. Shraddha-traya-vibhaga-Yoga**.‚ÄîArjuna asks ShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa to describe what is the essence of all true action and what is not, also what characterises all untrue action, according as the predominant motive-nature is S√¢ttvika, R√¢jasa, or t√¢masa.

** CHAP: XVIII*. Sannyasa-Yoga*.**‚ÄîShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa sums up, finally, the whole teaching regarding the combination of renunciation of fruit of action with performance, which is the only means of secur-

ing ultimate happiness‚ÄîArjuna’s doubts are solved; he is convinced and satisfied, and expresses his readiness to do his duty of battling with the unrighteous.

APPENDIX.

INDEX.

INTRODUCTION.

T

HIS edition of the Bhagavad-GiÃÇt√¢ has been prepared for the use of those who, while studying this Hindu scripture mainly for the sake of its priceless teachings, wish, being little acquainted with Samsk·πõit, to utilise the text, thus gaining a fuller insight into the meaning than can be gained through a translation, and incidentally acquiring a better knowledge of that language also. A brief note on the grammar of Samskrit, putting before the reader afew of the most salient features thereof, is therefore added here, as likely to be of use. It will help by giving a bird‚Äôs eye view of the subject and thus some idea of how one part is connected with another. The details must of course be looked up in any large grammar, if needed.

I. Alphabet:‚ÄîA complete alphabet would comprise hundreds, perhaps thousands, of single sounds. Out of these each human race or sub-race uses a comparatively small number, selected in accordance with the constitution of its vocal organs and of other aspects of its physical and superphysical being. There is a correspondence between all the parts of an organism; and the means and instruments of manifestation possessed by a race or nation, as by an individual, are, generally speaking, in correspondence with the ‚Äòruling passion,‚Äô the ‚Äòmain idea,‚Äô which that race or nation embodies and has to express. These ‚Äòideas,‚Äô ‚Äòpassions,‚Äô ‚Äòemotions,‚Äô ‚Äòglories,‚Äô ‚Äòaspects,‚Äô ‚Äòmodifications,‚Äô

of the Universal Self, Spirit, or Consciousness, are infinite; the world-process which endeavours to express them is infinite. One main idea (others being subordinate) is expressed by one individual, or one race, in any one time and space, ‚Äòbeauty‚Äô, ‚Äòstrength,‚Äô ‚Äôcomfort‚Äô, ‚Äòpeace,‚Äô ‚Äòwar‚Äô, ‚Äòscience,‚Äô ‚Äòlaw‚Äô, ‚Äòduty‚Äô ‚Äòright,‚Äô ‚Äòpiety,‚Äô ‚Äòdevotion,‚Äô &c. &c. The various members of a race, which is the embodiment and exponent of any one such main idea, have to use means of communication with each other to intensify that exposition, to make their lives fuller and deeper. This means, during the present cycle of evolution, is sound-language. In other cycles it may be sight-language, or touch-language, or smell-language, &c. &c. This sound-language is made up of single sounds, which, as said before, are in accordance with the ‚Äòbody,‚Äô the physical constitution, of the race, which, again, is in accordance with its ‚Äòspirit,‚Äô its ruling idea. A race embodying sweetness and gentleness would unconsciously select the soft and sweet sounds for its language; another manifesting martial strength and spirit, the harsher and more definite ones.

The Sa·πÉskrit language, embodying Dharma, law and order, the instrument of a civilisation whose characteristics are systematisation, rounded comprehensiveness and completeness, an ordered arrangement of life from beginning to end, and in all departments, is therefore itself systematic. It uses fourteen vowels and thirty-five consonants, a total of seven sevens. Some put the nasal and the aspirate sounds‚Äî.anusv√°ra and: visarga‚Äîamongst the vowels, and so count them as sixteen and the consonants thirty-three. They are arranged systematically, according to the regions of the vocal apparatus whence they proceed, as gutturals, linguoe-radicals, palatals, cerebrals, dentals,

labials, and certain combinations of these‚Äîbeginning from the throat and proceeding outwards. As to why we have here two septenaries of vowels and five septenaries of consonants is a question which could possibly be answered by one who had sufficient knowledge of world-evolution to be able to say why the present human race has two hands and two feet and five senses of knowledge and five organs of action, and five fingers on each hand and five toes on each foot, &c. The question can only tantalise us. But we can vaguely see that if these sounds of the Sa·πÉskrit alphabet were multiplied systematically and permuted and combined, we should obtain a scheme which would cover all the languages of the earth, and bring into line all the very various-seeming sounds which constitute the several alphabets of these. F.i., taking the linguoe-radicals ‡§ï, ‡§ñ, ‡§ó, ‡§ò, ‡§ô, we find that ‡§ñ, is the aspirate of ‡§ï, that the guttural of this ‡§ñ, viz, ‡§ñ‡§º, kÃ±h, has been appropriated by the Persian race and language and has disappeared from Sa·πÉskrit; that ‡§ó, is only a deepening of ‡§ï; and that ‡§ò, is the aspirate of ‡§ó; that the guttural of ‡§ï i.e., q, and of , ‡§ó i.e, gÃ±h, again, have disappeared from Sa·πÉskrit and been appropriated by the Arabic race and language; that ‡§ô‡§º, is the nasal of ‡§ó, as now pronounced, and that the nasals of the other letters, ‡§ï, ‡§ñ, ‡§ò,&c., &c., are not present in Sa·πÉskrit, but may possibly be present in some of the so-called savage languages, as ‘snorts’ and ‘grants’ of various kinds.

This process of elimination, of the disappearance of sounds and letters, may be witnessed as being in progress even to-day. In Sa·πÉskrit the vowels, ‡§ã, ‡§ã‡•É, ‡§≤‡•É, ‡§≤‡•É‡•É have practically disappeared from use; their outer written form is preserved, but for all practical purposes of pronunciation they have merged into ‡§∞ and ‡§≤.

The distinction between ‡§∂ and ‡§∑ is on the wane; the probability, judging by the rules of pronunciation given by PaÃÇ·πái·πái, is that ‡§∑ in another form of the Persian kÃ±h mentioned above, as thediscarded·≥≤‡§ï is that of the Arabic qÃ± and the similarly ofabandoned ·≥≤‡§™the Persian p·∏• or f.

The nasal letters again ‡§ô, ‡§û, ‡§£, ‡§®, have a tendency to run, all of them, into the single ‡§®, in the months of those who are not particularly careful. So in English, sounds which originally existed when such words as ‘daughter’ (Persian dukÃ±htar, Sa·πÉskrit duhitaÃÇ), and ’night’ (Sa·πÉskrit nakta) &c. were brought over, have now disappeared except perhaps in some village dialects, though the spelling continues as before. The reason of this may be suggested thus. According to the variety of the aspects of consciousness embodied in a people will be the variety of sounds, or letters employed by them; as the one variety diminishes or expands so will the other: if the word laugh were pronounced as lawgÃ±h as it probably was in the beginning, and as it is still by ‘uneducated’ people, it would imply a greater and a somewhat coarser heartiness; pronounced as l√¢f it indicates a toning down of the uncontrolled animal spirit, into a much more mild and refined condition of feeling. In America, and in some English circles, it is becoming further shortened into laff.

There are only a few verses now current, as to the psychophysics of sound and language, in P√¢·πáini’s Shiksh√¢, and these are not very intelligible in the absence of full explanations. They may be summarised thus: The Self cognising facts by means of the Buddhi (Reason) inspires the Manas (intelligence) with the desire to speak; the Manas strikes the k√¢y√¢gni-the fire of the body; and it in turn sets in motion the m√¢ruta, the wind or breath that moving in the chest generates the Mandra sound; that, again, rising to the palate (or the crown of the head) and rebounding thence passes to the mouth and produces articulate sounds, classified according to tone, time, place, effort, &c., &c.

II. Sandhi or Coalescence of sounds or letters‚ÄîIn connection with these letter sounds, arises the first difficulty of Sa·πÉskrit Grammar, Sandhi, the coalescence of sounds. When one word is followed by another, in quick succession of pronunciation, the natural tendency is for the tail of the first and the head of the second to get mixed up: This mixture sometimes takes a shape which, while no doubt always retaining traces of the compo-

nents, is not always analysable into these components by the beginner. The coalescence takes place in all languages, and offers one of the chief difficulties to the foreigner; but in other languages, for the most part, no attempt is made to express in writing the spoken sound. Thus the Frenchman says ‚Äúle’pti,‚Äù but he always writes ‚Äúle petit.‚Äù In Sa·πÉskrit writing the spoken sound is imitated. The detailed rules on this subject, which two sounds mixing make up which third sound, are many. The generalised rule may be stated to be that if the student will pronounce the two letters correctly and quickly in succession, the resultant sound, compelled by his own vocal apparatus, will also be in accordance with the grammatical rule on the subject. Briefly, the grammatical rule is only a description of the physiological fact. This appears not only in the case of actual coalescence, but in the influence of one sound on another separated from it by one or more intervening ones. Thus, according to the ordinary rule the word ‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ declined into its instrumental form, should be ‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡•á‡§®, but after pronouncing the cerebral r in its exact Sa·πÉskrit shade, the vocal apparatus of the Indian finds it difficult to adjust itself rapidly to pronounce the dental-nasal n‡§® properly, even when another labial nasal m intervenes; it turns more readily to the allied and nearer cerebral-nasal ·πá, ‡§£, and the result is ‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡•á‡§£.

Examples:

(a.) Conjunction of similar rowels: whether both short or both long, or one short and one long, the resultant is the long vowel.

(d) Consonants : The final consonant of one word is modified by the first consonant of the succeeding one. These changes are very numerous and intricate, and will only gradually become familiar; the following cases show the general principle above mentioned that the writing follows the sound; ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ = ‡§Ø‡§ú‡•ç‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ (vii.2.), ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡§§‡•ç = ‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡§§‡•ç (x. 41.), ‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§ö = ‡§Ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö (x. 39.)

III. Compounds. When all cases of simple sandhi have been eliminated, the student is faced with another difficulty; words are compounded together, and only the last word of the compound is complete; the remaining word or words have their endings clipped off, and these have to be supplied before

the compound becomes intelligible. Such compounds are called ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∏. They are of six chief kinds.

‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§µcompounds; ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§É ‡§ö ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡§É ‡§ö = ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ó‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡•å (iii. 34.); ‡§™‡§£‡§µ‡§æ‡§É ‡§ö ‡§Ü‡§®‡§ï‡§æ‡§É ‡§ö ‡§ó‡•ã‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§æ‡§É ‡§ö = ‡§™‡§£‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§ï‡§ó‡•ã‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ñ‡§æ‡§É (i. 13.)

‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑ compounds ; ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ ‡§à‡§∂ = ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡•á‡§∂ (xi.45); ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Ø‡•á‡§® ‡§Ü‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§É = ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§É (xi.14.)

‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§ó‡•Å Compounds ; ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§É=‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç(ii. 45.)

‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§µ‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§π‡§ø Compounds ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§á‡§Ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§É = ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡§ø‡§§‡•á‡§Ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§É (vi.8.); ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§É ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§§‡•ç = ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡•ç‡§∂‡§Æ‡•ç (vi. 28.)

‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡•Ä‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ Compounds ; ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§É ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡•á = ‡§Ö‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§

To solve these compounds it will be noticed that a knowledge of case-endings is necessary; it is the case-ending which is struck out in a compound, and the meaning of the compound depends on this eliminated case-ending. Hence the next step of the learner is to familiarise himself with the case-endings, of which every noun has twenty-one-7 cases in 3 numbers, singular, dnal and plural.

IV. Words‚Äî(a) Declension of nouns.Wordsare mainly oftwo kinds, names of things and names of actions, corresponding respectively to the cognition and action aspects of consciousness. Mediating between the two, binding them together, as desire binds cognition and action, is the third kind of word, the preposition; it forms a separate word in the modern languages, for various evolutionary reasons; but in the Sa·πÉskrit, it is hidden in and is a part of the noun itself, in the shape of the declensional termination, even as desire is hidden in theperson, the actor, and is not something separate from him. Other

parts of speech are off-shoots from these three principal ones. Nouns including prononns are (i) simple, subdivided by gender into 3 classes, or (ii) derivative, i. e. to say, derived from verb-roots. Of course one theory, and probably the right one, is that all names or nouns are ultimately connected with and founded on some characteristic action of the object; but without going into, the detail of the theory, it is enough for practical purposes to recognise that as the Sa·πÉskrit language now stands, some of its nouns may be regarded as, on their face, simple and some as derivative. To denote the kind of relation that exists between one noun and another, and also to give some indication of the kind of action passing between them, is the purpose of prepositional, or declensional, terminations. As pre-positions, distinguishing and separating marks of the kinds of the nouns concerned, showing the position or situation or case in which they are, whether nominative, or accusative, or instrumental, &c., with reference to each other, these declensional terminations are called vibhaktis; as indicating that some action passes between them, they are called k√¢rakas.

(b) Adjectives and adverbs &c. Adjectives, words indicative of the ‚Äòqualities‚Äô of nouns, are declined in the same way as the latter, and when not compounded with these are always in ‚Äúapposition‚Äù with them.

Adverbs are mostly ‚ÄòIndeclinable‚Äô in Sa·πÉskrit. These indeclinables include also some words, however, which are not adverbs; thus conjunctions and interjections are included here. Examples ‡§ö, ‡§Ö‡§§‡§ø, ‡§Ö‡§§‡•Ä‡§µ, ‡§Ö‡§•. ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§§‡§É, ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç, ‡§Ö‡§π‡•ã‡§¨‡§§, ‡§Ö‡§π‡•ã, ‡§á‡§§‡§ø, ‡§á‡§µ, ‡§á‡§π, ‡§â‡§§, ‡§è‡§µ, ‡§è‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç, ‡§ï‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç ‡§ï‡§•‡§Æ‡•ç, ‡§ï‡§ø‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•Å, ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•á, ‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡•ç, ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ, ‡§§‡•Å, ‡§®, &c., &c., are of frequent occurrence.

(c) Gender. The marks of gender are very perplexed in Sa·πÉskrit: They belong to the noun only, as in most languages; and not the verb, as in Hindi, Arabic or French. As a general rule, governing frequent cases, when the male base ends in a short ‡§Ö, the corresponding female base ends in the long ‡§Ü; and the neuter in ‡§Ö‡§Ç.

(d) Verbs. After nouns, verbs. The list of roots given by Pa·πáini numbers 2343; and each is capable of undergoing hundreds of inflectional or conjugational terminations, on account of moods and tenses, and causative, desiderative and repetitive forms of it, and all these again can be conjugated in the active or the passive form, and so on.But by the same processes of selection and elimination that havegoverned the numberof the letters, the verb-roots in actual use in current Sa·πÉskrit are not many more than 500 and of these a very limited number of forms is used.

The tenses and moods are taken together and all called vibhaktis or lak√°ras; and there are ten of such, viz., six tenses and four moods. The tenses are, 1 present; 3 past; and 2 future; and the moods are Imperative, Potential, Benedictive and

Conditional. In common and unclassical Sa·πÉskrit, one form of the past, viz., the first preterite, is largely used, the other two being very infrequent; so the first future is seldom met with even in classical Sa·πÉskrit. Of the moods, the benedictive mood is very rarely used.

If the whole list of the 2343 roots were carefully examined, very many more roots would be found to be common between the several AÃÇryan languages than are now noted. But, as said before in connection with the alphabet, the genius of each later language has selected out of the roots what were most suited to it, and the older race, the root-race, has correspondingly dropped them in its later career. It is as if there had been a partition of patrimony between heirs so that what one took the others could not retain.

These verbs are divided into ten classes; the external reason is that the verbs of each class resemble each other more than those of the other classes, in the general appearance of their conjugational forms. But this again requires a deeper why; and that is not traceable. If it could be said that there were ten main kinds of human action, we should have approximated to the answer wanted.

There are two main forms of conjugation, the ‡§™‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•à-‡§™‡§¶ ‚Äúfor another‚Äù; and the Tre ‚Äú‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡•á-‡§™‡§¶‚Äù ‚Äúfor self‚Äù. The former, generally speaking, belongs to the active voice, wherein one person does something to another; and the latter to the passive voice, wherein something is done ‚Äòto one self‚Äô. But while in the passive voice formof any verb only the ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡•á‡§™‡§¶ form is used, in the active voice the‡§™‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡•à‡§™‡§¶ form is usedonly generally, and, sometimes the‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡•á‡§™‡§¶ form is used,and in some cases both.

The following forms occur frequently in the G√Æt√¢.

[TABLE]

[TABLE]

In the technical works on grammar, rules are given for the correct use of all the ten moods and tenses; thus the first preterite should be used when the event is older than the current day; the 2nd when it occurred outside the speaker’s personal knowledge; and the 3rd in any case; and so the 1st future should be used when the event is expected very shortly. But these rules, as said before, are observed only in classical composition. There is also some reason to think that they do not represent accurately the real original distinctions. It is possible that the three pasts corresponded to the English ‘imperfect’ and ‘pluperfect’ &c.

The addition of prefixes entirely changes the meaning of the verb-root, as stated in the classical shloka on the subject.

‡§â‡§™‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡•á‡§£ ‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ã‡§¨‡§≤‡§æ‡§¶‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§®‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡•§
‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡§Ç‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§µ‡§ø‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡••

‚ÄúThe prefex violently drags away elsewhere the meaning of the verb-root; thus, with the common root ‡§π‡•É, to take away, ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§π‡§æ‡§∞ means a blow ‡§Ü‡§π‡§æ‡§∞ food, ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§π‡§æ‡§∞ destruction ‡§µ‡§ø‡§π‡§æ‡§∞, play, and ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§π‡§æ‡§∞ clearing away‚Äù. A careful analysis shows that the idea of taking away is present in some shape or other throughout all these.

Each verb-root is capable of being modified into a causative form, a desiderative form and a frequentative form; and each of these forms is capable of independent conjugation in all moods and tenses.

Causative; The causative verb is formed by the addition of ‡§á, which becomes when the termination is added ‡§Ø, ‡§ò‡§æ‡§§‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø (ii. 21) he causes to kill, from ‡§π‡§®‡•ç to kill; ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ú‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø(iii.1) thou causest (me) to engage, from ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ú‡•ç to join;‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á (iii.5) he is caused to do, from ‡§ï‡•É to do; ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ö‡§æ‡§≤‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡•ç (iii. 29) he may cause to be unsettled, from ‡§ö‡§≤‡•çto move; ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∏‡§æ‡§¶‡§Ø‡•á‡§§‡•çlet (him) cause to sink down, from‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∏‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç a down. to be cast down.

** Desiderative; The desiderative verb expresses the wish of the agent; ‡§∏ is added, and the first letter is reduplicated ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§µ‡§É (i.i) wishing to fight, from ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ß‡•çto fight; ‡§ú‡§ø‡§ú‡•Ä ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§æ‡§Æ‡§É (ii. 6) (we) wish to from ‡§ú‡•Ä‡§µ‡•ç to live, ‡§ö‡§ø‡§ï‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡•Å‡§É(iii. 25) desiring to do, from ‡§ï‡•É to do; ‡§Ü‡§∞‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ã‡§É(of the) wishing to ascend, from‡§Ü‡§∞‡•Å‡§π‡•ç** to mount.

*(e)*Derivative words. Derivative words of various kinds, e,g. some indicating moods not covered by the regular conjugatious, are formed by means of affixes.

V. Sentences. The two main forms of the sentence are : (1) that in the active voice, and (2) that in the passive.

(1) Active : ‡§® ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§≤‡§ø‡§™‡§Ç‡§§‡§ø (iv. 14) = Actions do not stain me.

(2) Passive : ‡§≤‡§ø‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á ‡§® ‡§∏ ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡•á‡§® (v. 10) = He is not stained by sin.

In construing a shloka the first thing to do is to separate out the words, so as to give to each its complete and independent existence ; the coalesced tails and heads [ ‡§∏‡§®‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø] are first dissolved into their component parts; then the compound words are separated, and any lacking case-endings supplied; then the words are arranged in their prose order. In Sa·πÉskrit order matters comparatively little, but still there is more inversion permissible in poetry than in prose. Let us take i. 2, 3.

‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§§‡•Å ‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç‡§°‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§¢‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ß‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§¶‡§æ‡•§
‡§Ü‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§™‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§µ‡§ö‡§®‡§Æ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç‡••

Separated into independent words:

The prose-order :

** ‡§§‡§¶‡§æ ‡§∞‡§æ‡§ú‡§æ ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ß‡§®‡§É ‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç‡§°‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Ä‡§ï‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Å ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§¢‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ü‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§â‡§™‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ó‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§µ‡§ö‡§®‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§Ö‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§µ‡•Ä‡§§‡•ç‡••**

‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡•à‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç‡§°‡•Å‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§
‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§¢‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§™‡§¶‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§§‡§µ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§£ ‡§ß‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§§‡§æ‡••

Separated into independent words:

‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§è‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç (‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç‡§°‡•Å‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç = ) ‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç‡§°‡•ã‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§Ü‡§ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§

** ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§¢‡§æ‡§Ç (‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§™‡§¶‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£ = ) ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§™‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§§‡§µ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§£ ‡§ß‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§§‡§æ‡••**

The English prose-order of this would run.

‡§π‡•á ‡§Ü‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§è‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç‡§°‡•ã‡§É‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§¢‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§µ ‡§ß‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§£ ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§™‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£‡••

But in Sa·πÉskrit prose-order:

** ‡§π‡•á ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§ö‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§µ ‡§ß‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§§‡§æ ‡§∂‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§£ ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§™‡§¶‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Ç‡§¢‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§è‡§§‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§™‡§æ‡§Ç‡§°‡•ã‡§É ‡§™‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§§‡•Ä‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§Æ‡•Ç‡§Æ‡•ç ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡••**

‡•• ‡§Ö‡§• ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§Æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§≠‡§ó‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§ó‡•Ä‡§§‡§æ ‡••

‡§Ö‡§• ‡§ó‡•Ä‡§§‡§æ‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§

‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§ó‡§£‡•á‡§∂‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§®‡§Æ‡§É‡•• ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∞‡§æ‡§ß‡§æ‡§∞‡§Æ‡§£‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§®‡§Æ‡§É ‡•• ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ã‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö ‡•• ‡§≠‡§ó‡§µ‡§®‡•ç‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•á‡§∂‡§æ‡§® ‡§™‡§æ‡§†‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Ω‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡•á ‡§§‡§§‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡§Ç‡§™‡§æ‡§†‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ö‡§∞‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡••

‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§≠‡§æ‡§ó‡§Ç ‡§™‡§†‡§Æ‡§æ‡§®‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å ‡§ó‡§Ç‡§ó‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§´‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§≤‡§≠‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡••

‡§è‡§ï‡§æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§§‡•Å ‡§Ø‡•ã ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§™‡§†‡§§‡•á ‡§≠‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§§‡§É‡••

‡§è‡§§‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§π‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§Ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ó‡•Ä‡§§‡§æ‡§≠‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ã‡§§‡§ø ‡§Ø‡§É‡••

THE BHAGAVAD-GIÃÇTAÃÇ.

THE GREATNESS OF THE GIÃÇTAÃÇ.

Hail to ShriÃÇ Ga·πáe·π£ha! Hail to the Lover of RaÃÇdhaÃÇ !

DharaÃÇ (the Earth) said: O Blessed One, O Supreme Ruler, how may unfaltering devotion arise in him who is constrained by pr√¢rabdha karma, O Lord?

(1). Vi·π£h·πáu said : He who, constrained by praÃÇrabdha karma, is ever assiduously practising the G√Æt√¢, he is liberated, he is happy in this world, he is not stained bain by karma.

(2). If he study the G√Æt√¢, then can no sin stain him, as water stains not the lotus-leaf.

(3). Where is the book of the G√Æt√¢, where its reading is performed, there are all holy places, there indeed Pray√¢ga and the rest.

(4). All Devas, ·πöi·π£his, YogiÃÇs and Pannagas, also GopaÃÇlas and GopiÃÇs also, with N√¢rada, Uddhava, and their train.

(5). Help comes swiftly where the G√Æt√¢ is recited. Where the discussion of the G√Æt√¢, the recitation, the teaching, of it is heard, there I, O Earth, doubtless ever abide.

(6). I dwell in the retreat of the G√Æt√¢; the G√Æt√¢ is My best abode. Having mastered the wisdom of the G√Æt√¢, I protect the three worlds.

(7). The G√Æt√¢ is My supreme science, it is verily the form of Brahman. It is the half-syllable eternal (the dot on the ‡•ê), everlasting, the ineffable essence of the Self,

(8). Spoken by K·πõi·π£h·πáa, the Omniscient, the Blessed, to Arjuna with His own mouth, the three Vedas, the final bliss, the exposition of the knowledge of the Tattvas.

(9). The man who constantly repeats the eighteen discourses with unwavering mind, he will obtain perfect wisdom and reach the supreme goal.

(10). If the complete recitation be impossible, then may one half be read. Then he doubtless obtains merit, equal to the gift of a cow.

(11). By the reading of a third part, he may obtain

the fruit of bathing in GangaÃÇ. By the repetition of a sixth part, he may obtain the fruit of the Soma-sacrifice.

(12). He who daily reads a single discourse with devotion, he, having become a Ga·πáa, obtains Rudraloka and therein long abides.

(13). The man who daily reads a quarter-shloka, he, O Earth, remains a man during a manvantara.

(14). The man who should read ten, seven, five, four, two, three, one, or half a shloka of the G√Æt√¢,

(15). He securely obtains Chandraloka for ten thousand years. Ever engaged in the reading of the G√Æt√¢, passing through death, he would return to humanity.

(16). Having again practised the G√Æt√¢, he obtains the supreme liberation. Engaged in the utterance, ‚ÄúG√Æt√¢,‚Äù the dying reaches the path.

(17). Attached to the hearing of the G√Æt√¢, even the great sinner attains to Vaikuntha, and rejoices with Vi·π£h·πáu.

(18). He who meditates on the meaning of the G√Æt√¢, having performed actions abundantly, he should be known as a J√Ævanmukta, and at the ending of the body reaches the supreme goal.

(19). Having taken refuge in the G√Æt√¢, many Kings,

Janaka and the rest, praised in the world, being cleansed from sins, have gone to the supreme goal.

(20). Having accomplished the reading of the G√Æt√¢, he who should not also read this description of its greatness, his reading is in vain, and his labour is lost.

(21). He who performs the practice of the G√Æt√¢, associated with this discourse on its Greatness, he obtains the fruit, he may reach the path difficult of obtainment.

(22). S√ªta said: He who should read this eternal Greatness of the G√Æt√¢, proclaimed by me, at the end of the G√Æt√¢, should obtain that fruit which has been described.

(23). Thus in the blessed V√¢r√¢hapur√¢·πáa the greatness of the G√Æt√¢ is set forth.

‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî

Note. The statements made above, as to the value of reading portions, or the whole, of the G√Æt√¢, may seem at first sight to be somewhat fantastic. They should not be construed as meaning the mere reading, the lip-repetition, of verses, but rather as the mastering and assimilation of the spirit of the G√Æt√¢, and the life-repetition thereof. The man who has thus wrought the G√Æt√¢ into his life is a JiÃÇvanmukta, and the assimilation of one shloka, of several, of a quarter of the G√Æt√¢, and so on, mark various stages in human evolution, each of which has its own appropriate fruit.

THE ARRANGEMENT OF THE HANDS ETC.

FOR THE GIÃÇTAÃÇ.

‡•ê The Blessed Veda-Vy√¢sa is the ·πúi·π£hi of this Bhagavad-GiÃÇtaÃÇ mantra. The metre is the anu·π£h·π≠up. ShriÃÇ K·πõi·π£h·πáa, the Supreme Self, is its Devat√¢. ‚ÄúThou grievest for those that should not be grieved for, yet speakest words of wisdom:‚Äù such is the Bija. ‚ÄúAbandoningall duties, come unto me alone for shelter: ‚Äù such is the Shakti. ‚ÄúI will liberate thee from all sins; sorrow not:‚Äù such is the KiÃÇlakam. ‚ÄúWeapons cleave him not, nor fire burneth him;‚Äù thus:‚ÄúTo the two thumbs, all hail!‚Äù ‚ÄúNor waters wet him, nor wind drieth him away;‚Äù thus: ‚ÄúTo the two index fingers, all hail!‚Äù ‚ÄúUncleavable he, incombustible he, neither to be wetted nor dried away;‚Äù thus: ‚ÄúTo the two middle fingers, all hail!‚Äù ‚ÄúPerpetual, all-pervasive, stable, immovable, ancient;‚Äù thus: ‚ÄúTo the two nameless (fourth) fingers, all hail!‚Äù ‚ÄúBehold, O P√¢rtha, my Forms,) a hundred-fold, a thousand-fold;‚Äù thus: ‚ÄúTo the two little fingers, all hail!‚Äù ‚ÄúVarious in kind, divine, various in colours;‚Äù thus: ‚ÄúTo the two palms and backs of the

hands, all hail!‚Äù Such is the disposition of the hands. Now as to the disposition of the heart and the other organs. ‚ÄúWeapons cleave him not, nor fire burneth him;‚Äù thus: ‚ÄúTo the heart, all hail !‚Äù ‚ÄúNor waters wet him, nor wind drieth him away;‚Äù thus: ‚ÄúTo the head, svaÃÇhaÃÇ!‚Äù Uncleavable he, incombustible he, neither to be wetted nor dried away;‚Äù thus: ‚ÄúTo the shikhaÃÇ, vashat!‚Äù ‚ÄúPerpetual, all-pervasive, stable, immovable;‚Äù thus: ‚ÄúTo the encasement, huh!‚Äù ‚ÄúBehold, O PaÃÇrtha, my Forms, a hundred-fold, a thousand-fold;‚Äù thus: ‚ÄúTo the fire-weapon, phat!‚Äù For the propitiation of ShriÃÇ Kri·π£h·πáa the reading is enjoined.

‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî‚Äî

Note. A mantra, in the Hindu religion, has a Rishi, who gave it ; A metre, which governs the inflection of the voice. A DevataÃÇ‚Äîa supernatural being, higher or lower‚Äîas its informing power. The BiÃÇja, seed, is a significant word, or series of words, which gives it a special power; sometimes this word is a sound which harmonises with the key-note of the individual using it, and varies with the individual; sometimes this word expresses the essence of the mantra, and the result of the mantra is the flower springing from this seed; this essence of the GiÃÇtaÃÇ is in the words quoted. The Shakti is the energy of the form of the mantra, i. e. the vibration-forms set up by its sounds; these in the G√Æt√¢ carry the man to the Lord. The Kilakam, the pillar, is that which supports and makes strong the mantra; this is the ceasing of sorrow, by the freeing from imperfections.

Then follow certain special directions, intended to set up relations between the centres in the man’s body and the corresponding centres in the body of the Lord‚Äîthe universe. The thumbs, the earth-symbol, are connected with the physical plane, and are utilised in control of its subtle forces. The index fingers, the water-symbol, are con-

nected with the astral plane, and are similarly used with astral forces. The middle fingers, the fire-symbol, the apex of the pyramid of the hand, are similarly used with mental forces. The fourth fingers, the air-symbol, are similarly used with buddhic forces. The little fingers, the most powerful in the subtle worlds, are the √¢k√¢sha-symbol, and are similarly used with √¢tmic forces. The other organs of the body are the other poles of the magnet of the body: thumbs and heart; index fingers and head; middle fingers and the point of juncture of the occipital and parietal sutures; the fourth fingers and the s√ªkshma shariÃÇra; the little fingers and the creative organ. These are the positive and negative poles of the magnet we call the body; either may be positive, either negative, according to the object aimed at, but they work together.

From other stand-points, these dispositions may be regarded as having on the one hand, an inhibitory, or, on the other, a stimulative effect on the physical or subtler function of the organs concerned, according to the purpose in view in the subsequent meditation or practice.

To the western world these correspondences may seem fantastic and superstitious; to the eastern world, in which the faint tradition of the Great Science lingers, they sound as echoes of a mightier age, when Gods and Men walked familiarly together in the Hidden Ways.

THE MEDITATION ON GITA.

‡•ê Taught by the Blessed NaÃÇraÃÇya·πáaHimself to Arjuna; compiled by VyaÃÇsa, the ancient Muni, in the midst of the MahaÃÇbhaÃÇrata; I meditate on thee, O Mother, O Bhagavad-G√Æt√¢, the blessed, the pourer-forth of the nectar of Advaita, destroyer of rebirth. Hail to thee, O VyaÃÇsa, large-minded one, with eyes like the petals of the opened lotus; by whom the lamp of knowledge, filled with the oil of the MahaÃÇbhaÃÇrata, has been lighted. To the P√¢rij√¢ta-kalpataru of the suppliant, to the holder of the whip, to Kri·π£h·πáa as Teacher, milker of the G√Æt√¢-nectar, all hail! All the Upani·π£hats are the cows; the Milker is the joy of the cowherds; P√¢rtha is the calf; the man of high intelligence is the enjoyer; the milk is the great nectar of the G√Æt√¢. I worship VaÃÇsudeva, the charioteer, the God, the destroyer of Kamsa and Ch√¢·πá≈´ÃÇra, the supreme joy of DevakiÃÇ, Kri·π£h·πáa, Guru of the world. With Keshava as helmsman, verily was crossed by the PaÃÇ·πá·∏çavas that

battle-river, whose banks were BhiÃÇ·π£hma and Dro·πáa, whose water was Jayadratha, whose blue lotus was G√¢ndh√¢ra, whose crocodile was Shalya, whose current was Kripa, whose billows were Kar·πáa, whose terrible alligators were Asvatth√¢m√¢ and Vikar·πáa, whose whirlpool was Duryodhana. May this Lotus of the Mah√¢bh√¢rata be for our good, born on the lake of the words of P√¢r√¢sharya, sweet with the fragrance of the meaning of the G√Æt√¢, pollened with many stories, unfolding to the sun of the history of Hari, sucked by the bees of good men in the world day by day, cleanser of the stains of Kali. I salute Him, M√¢dhava, the supreme Bliss, whose grace makes the dumb man eloquent, and the cripple a climber of mountains. Whom Brahm√¢, Varu·πáa, Indra, Rudra, and the Maruts praise with divine songs; whom the S√¢ma-chanters sing with the Vedas and with A·πÖgas, in the pada and krama methods with the Upani·π£hats; whom YogiÃÇs see by the mind fixed in meditation, to Him going; whose end Suras and Asuras know not; to that God, all hail!

‡§Ö‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§≠‡§ø‡§≠‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç = ‡§Ö‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§Ö‡§≠‡§ø‡§≠‡§µ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç of lawlessness, from prevalence; ‡§ï‡•É‡§∑‡•ç‡§£ 0 Kri·π£h·πáa; ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§§‡§ø become wicked; ‡§ï‡•Å‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§É = ‡§ï‡•Å‡§≤‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§É of the family, women; ‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•Ä‡§∑‡•Å in (the state of) women; ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§æ‡§∏‡•Å (being) wicked; ‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§£‡•á‡§Ø O VaÃÇr·π£h·πáeya; ‡§ú‡§æ‡§Ø‡§§‡•á arises; ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§É = ‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ï‡§∞‡§Éof caste, confusion.

This confusion draggeth to hell the slayers of the family and the family; for their ancestors fall, deprived of rice-balls and libations. (42)

Having thus spoken on the battle-field, Arjuna sank down on the seat of the chariot, casting away his bow and arrow, his mind overborne by grief. (47)

To him thus with pity overcome, with smarting brimming eyes, despondent, MadhusuÃÇdana spake these words : (1)

‡§§‡§Ç to him; ‡§§‡§•‡§æ thus; ‡§ï‡•É‡§™‡§Ø‡§æ with pity; ‡§Ü‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Æ‡•ç penetrated; ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡§æ‡§ï‡•Å‡§≤‡•á‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡§Æ‡•ç = ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§≠‡§ø‡§É ‡§™‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§£‡•á ‡§ö ‡§Ü‡§ï‡•Å‡§≤‡•á ‡§ö ‡§à‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§£‡•á ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§§‡§Ç‡§É with tears, filled, and, restless (smarting), and, (two) eyes, whose, him; ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•Ä‡§¶‡§§‡§Ç (to the) despondent; ‡§á‡§¶‡§Ç this; ‡§µ‡§æ‡§ï‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç speech; ‡§â‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö spake; ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•Å‡§∏‡•Ç‡§¶‡§®‡§É MadhusuÃÇdana.

The Blessed Lord said:

Whence hath this dejection befallen thee in this perilous strait, ignoble, heaven-closing, infamous, O Arjuna? (2)

‡§ï‡•Å‡§§‡§É whence; ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æthee; ‡§ï‡§∂‡•ç‡§Æ‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç dejection; ‡§á‡§¶‡§Ç this; ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Æ‡•á in difficulty; ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•Å‡§™‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§ø‡§§‡§Æ‡•çhappened; ‡§Ö‡§®‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ü‡§Æ‡•ç = ‡§Ö‡§®‡§æ-

Yield not to impotence, O P√¢rtha! it doth not befit thee. Shake off this paltry faint-heartedness ! Stand up, Parantapa ! (3)

The Blessed Lord said:

Thou grievest for those that should not be grieved for, yet speakest words of wisdom9. The wise grieve neither for the living nor for the dead.(11)

‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ã‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç unbewailable; ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§∂‡•ã‡§ö‡§É hast bewailed; ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Çthon; ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•ç = ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§ú‡•ç‡§û‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•ç of wisdom, words; ‡§öand; ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∑‡§∏‡•á speakest; ‡§ó‡§§‡§æ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§®‡•ç = ‡§ó‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§Ö‡§∏‡§µ‡§É ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡•ç gone, life-breaths, whose, those; ‡§Ö‡§ó‡§§‡§æ‡§∏‡•Ç‡§®‡•ç not, gone, life-breaths, whose, those; ‡§ö and; ‡§® not; ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§∂‡•ã‡§ö‡§§‡§ø grieve; ‡§™‡§Ç‡§°‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É the wise.

Nor at any time verily was I not, nor thou, nor these princes of men, nor verily shall we ever cease to be, hereafter. (12)

‡§® not; ‡§§‡•Å indeed; ‡§è‡§µ also;‡§Ö‡§Ç‡§π I; ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡•Å at any time; ‡§® not; ‡§Ü‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ç was; ‡§® not; ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç thou; ‡§® not; ‡§á‡§Æ‡•á these; ‡§ú‡§®‡§æ‡§ß‡§ø‡§™‡§æ‡§É = ‡§ú‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Ö‡§ß‡§ø‡§™‡§æ‡§É of the people, lords; ‡§® not; ‡§ö and; ‡§è‡§µ also; ‡§® not;‡§≠‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡§É shall be; ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á all; ‡§µ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç we; ‡§Ö‡§§‡§É from this (time)‡§™‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç after.

He who regardeth this as a slayer, and he who thinketh he is slain, both of them are ignorant. He slayeth not, nor is he slain. (19)

‡§µ‡•á‡§¶ knows; ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§æ‡§∂‡§ø‡§®‡§Ç indestructible; ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç perpetual; ‡§Ø‡§É who; ‡§è‡§®‡§Ç this; ‡§Ö‡§ú‡§Ç unborn; ‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç undiminishing; ‡§ï‡§•‡§Ç how; ‡§∏‡§É he (that); ‡§™‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å‡§∑‡§Éman; ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§• O PaÃÇrtha; ‡§ï‡§Ç whom; ‡§ò‡§æ‡§§‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø causes to be slain; ‡§π‡§Ç‡§§‡§ø kills; ‡§ï‡§Æ‡•ç whom.

As a man, casting off worn-out garments, taketh new

‡§Ö‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•á‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É uncleavable; ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Ç this; ‡§Ö‡§¶‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É incombustible; ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Ç this; ‡§Ö‡§ï‡•ç‡§≤‡•á‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É unwettable; ‡§Ö‡§∂‡•ã‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É undryable; ‡§è‡§µ also; ‡§ö and; ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Éperpetual; ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§ó‡§§‡§É = ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§®‡•ç ‡§ó‡§§‡§É into all, gone; ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§æ‡§£‡•Å‡§É stable; ‡§Ö‡§ö‡§≤‡§É immovable; ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Ç this; ‡§∏‡§®‡§æ‡§§‡§®‡§É ancient.

‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ã‡§Ω‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§ö‡§ø‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡•§
‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡•à‡§®‡§Ç ‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•Å‡§∂‡•ã‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∏‡§ø‡••‡•®‡•´‡••

Unmanifest, unthinkable, immutable, he is called ; therefore knowing him as such, thou shouldst not grieve. (25)

‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É unmanifest; ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Ç this; ‡§Ö‡§ö‡§ø‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Éinconceivable; ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Ç this; ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É immutable; ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Ç this; ‡§â‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á is called; ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç therefore; ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç thus; ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ having known; ‡§è‡§®‡§Ç this; ‡§® not; ‡§Ö‡§®‡•Å‡§∂‡•ã‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç to grieve; ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∏‡§ø(thon) oughtest.

‡§Ö‡§• ‡§ö‡•à‡§®‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•á ‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§
‡§§‡§•‡§æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•ã ‡§®‡•à‡§®‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ã‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∏‡§ø‡••‡•®‡•¨‡••

Or if thou thinkest of him as being constantly born and constantly dying, even then, Omighty-armed, thou shouldst not grieve. (26)

‡§Ö‡§• now; ‡§ö and; ‡§è‡§®‡§Ç this; ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§Ç = ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§Ç constantly, born; ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç perpetually; ‡§µ‡§æ or; ‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•á (thou) thinkest; ‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç dead; ‡§§‡§•‡§æ so; ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø also; ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç thou; ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•ã = ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•å ‡§¨‡§æ‡§π‡•Ç ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø (‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç) great (two) arms, whose, (O thou ); ‡§® not; ‡§è‡§®‡§Ç this; ‡§∂‡•ã‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç to grieve; ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∏‡§ø (thon) oughtest.

‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§π‡§ø ‡§ß‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡•ã ‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö‡•§
‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§Ω‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á ‡§® ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ã‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∏‡§ø‡••‡•®‡•≠‡••

For certain is death for the born, and certain is birth for the dead; therefore over the inevitable thou shouldst not grieve.] (27)

‡§ú‡§æ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø of the born; ‡§π‡§ø indeed;‡§ß‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡§É certain; ‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡•Å‡§É death; ‡§ß‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§µ‡§Ç certain; ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ birth; ‡§Æ‡•É‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø of the dead; ‡§ö and; ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç therefore; ‡§Ö‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á (in) inevitable; ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•á in matter; ‡§® not; ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç thou; ‡§∂‡•ã‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•çto grieve; ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∏‡§ø (thon) onghtest.

‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§§‡•§
‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§ø‡§ß‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ ‡§ï‡§æ ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§®‡§æ‡••‡•®‡•Æ‡••

Beings are unmanifest in their origin, manifest in their midmost state, O Bh√¢rata, unmanifest likewise are they in dissolution. What room then for lamentation? (28)

** ‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§¶‡•Ä‡§®‡§ø = ‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Ü‡§¶‡§ø‡§É ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•á** unmanifest, beginning, whose, they; ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø beings; ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡§ø = ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø manifest, middle, whose, they; ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§§ O BhaÃÇrat; ‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§®‡§ø‡§ß‡§®‡§æ‡§®‡§ø = ‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§Ç ‡§®‡§ø‡§ß‡§®‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø unmanifest, destruction, whose, they; ‡§è‡§µ also: ‡§§‡§§‡•ç‡§∞ there; ‡§ï‡§æ what; ‡§™‡§∞‡§ø‡§¶‡•á‡§µ‡§®‡§æ lamentation.

‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§§‡•ç‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø ‡§ï‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§¶‡•á‡§®-
‡§Æ‡§æ‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§•‡•à‡§µ ‡§ö‡§æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É‡•§
‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§ö‡•ç‡§ö‡•à‡§®‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É ‡§∂‡•É‡§£‡•ã‡§§‡§ø
‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ω‡§™‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§®‡§Ç ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶ ‡§® ‡§ö‡•à‡§µ ‡§ï‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡••‡•®‡•Ø‡••

As marvellous one regardeth him; as marvellous another speaketh thereof; as marvellous another heareth thereof ; yet having heard, none indeed understandeth. (29)

** ‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§§‡•ç** as wonderful; ‡§™‡§∂‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§øsees; ‡§ï‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç some one; ‡§è‡§®‡§Ç this; ‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§§‡•ç as wonderful; ‡§µ‡§¶‡§§‡§ø speaks; ‡§§‡§•‡§æ 80; ‡§è‡§µalso; ‡§ö and; ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É another; ‡§Ü‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§§‡•ç is wonderful; ‡§ö and; ‡§è‡§®‡§Ç this; ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É another; ‡§∂‡•É‡§£‡•ã‡§§‡§ø hear;‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ having heard; ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø even; ‡§è‡§®‡§Ç this; ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶ knows; ‡§® not; ‡§ö and; ‡§è‡§µ also; ‡§ï‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•ç anyone.

‡§¶‡•á‡§π‡•Ä ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Æ‡§µ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§¶‡•á‡§π‡•á ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§§‡•§
‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§® ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ã‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∏‡§ø‡••‡•©‡•¶‡••

This dweller in the body of everyone is ever invulnerable, O BhaÃÇrata; therefore thou shouldst not grieve for any creature. (30)

‡§¶‡•á‡§π‡•Ä the embodied; ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç always; ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É invulnerable; ‡§Ö‡§Ø‡§Ç this; ‡§¶‡•á‡§π‡•á in the body; ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø of all; ‡§≠‡§æ‡§∞‡§§ O BhaÃÇrata; ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç therefore; ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø (for) all; ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø creatures; ‡§® not; ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç thou;‡§∂‡•ã‡§ö‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç to grieve; ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∏‡•á (thou) shouldst.

‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§ö‡§æ‡§µ‡•á‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§® ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ï‡§Ç‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∏‡§ø‡•§
‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Ø‡•ã‡§Ω‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§® ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡••‡•©‡•ß‡••

Further, looking to thine own duty, thou shouldst not tremble; for there is nothing more welcome to a K·π£hattriya than righteous war. (31)

‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç = ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Çof own, duty; ‡§Ö‡§™‡§ø also; ‡§ö and; ‡§Ö‡§µ‡•á‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø having seen; ‡§® not ; ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ï‡§Ç‡§™‡§ø‡§§‡•Å‡§Æ‡•ç to tremble; ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§π‡§∏‡§ø(thou) oughtest; ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç (than) righteous; ‡§π‡§ø indeed; ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•ç than war; ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•á‡§Ø‡§É better; ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•ç other; ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø of the K·π£hattriya; ‡§® not; ‡§µ‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á is.

‡§Ø‡§¶‡•É‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§ö‡•ã‡§™‡§™‡§®‡•ç‡§®‡§Ç ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Æ‡§™‡§æ‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡§Æ‡•ç ‡•§
‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§ø‡§®‡§É ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§≤‡§≠‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§Æ‡•Ä‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡§Æ‡•ç ‡••‡•©‡•®‡••

Happy the K·π£hattriyas, O P√¢rtha, who obtain such a fight, offered unsought as an open door to heaven. (32)

‡§Ø‡§¶‡•É‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§Ø‡§æ by chance; ‡§ö and;‡§â‡§™‡§™‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§Ç happened; ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Æ‡•ç = ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§∞‡§Ç of heaven, door; ‡§Ö‡§™‡§æ‡§µ‡•É‡§§‡§Ç opened; ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§ø‡§®‡§É happy; ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡§ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É k·π£hattriyas; ‡§™‡§æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§• O P√¢rtha; ‡§≤‡§≠‡§®‡•ç‡§§‡•á obtain; ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§Æ‡•ç battle; ‡§à‡§¶‡•É‡§∂‡§Æ‡•ç like this.

‡§Ö‡§• ‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§ß‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§® ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø ‡•§
‡§§‡§§‡§É ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Ç ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§Æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø ‡•• ‡•©‡•© ‡••

But if thou wilt not carry on this righteous warfare, then, casting away thine own duty and thine honour, thou wilt incur sin. (33)

‡§Ö‡§• now; ‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡•ç if; ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç thou; ‡§á‡§Æ‡§Æ‡•ç this; ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç righteous, ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ó‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§Æ‡§Ç battle; ‡§® not; ‡§ï‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø(thou) wilt do; ‡§§‡§§‡§É then; ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§ß‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§Çown duty; ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç fame; ‡§ö and; ‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ having thrown away; ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§Ç sin; ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø(thou) wilt obtain.

‡§Ö‡§ï‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§Ç ‡§ö‡§æ‡§™‡§ø ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø ‡§ï‡§•‡§Ø‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡•á‡§Ω‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§
‡§∏‡§Ç‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö‡§æ‡§ï‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§¶‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡••‡•©‡•™‡••

Men will recount thy perpetual dishonor, and, to one highly esteemed, dishonor exceedeth death. (34)

‡§Ö‡§ï‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§Çdishonor; ‡§ö and; ‡§Ö‡§™‡§øalso; ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§®‡§ø creatures; ‡§ï‡§•‡§Ø‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§§‡§ø will relate; ‡§§‡•á thy; ‡§Ö‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ø‡§æ‡§Æ‡•ç undiminishing; ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§≠‡§æ‡§µ‡§ø‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Øof the honored; ‡§ö and; ‡§Ö‡§ï‡•Ä‡§∞‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§É dishonor; ‡§Æ‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§§‡•ç than death; ‡§Ö‡§§‡§ø‡§∞‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á exceeds.

‡§≠‡§Ø‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§¶‡•Å‡§™‡§∞‡§§‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§§‡•á ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§•‡§æ‡§É‡•§
‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§Æ‡§§‡•ã ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø ‡§≤‡§æ‡§ò‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç‡••‡•©‡•´‡••

The great car-warriors will think thee fled from the battle from fear, and thou that wast highly thought of by them, wilt be It be lightly held. (35)

‡§≠‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç from fear; ‡§∞‡§£‡§æ‡§§‡•ç from the battle; ‡§â‡§™‡§∞‡§§‡§Ç withdrawn; ‡§Æ‡§Ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§§‡•á will think; ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ‡§Ç thee; ‡§Æ‡§π‡§æ‡§∞‡§•‡§æ‡§É the great car-warriors; ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç of whom; ‡§ö and; ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç thou; ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å much; ‡§Æ‡§§‡§É thought (of); ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ having been ; ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§∏‡§øwilt go; ‡§≤‡§æ‡§ò‡§µ‡§Æ‡•ç to lightness.

‡§Ö‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§Ç‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö ‡§¨‡§π‡•Å‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§§‡§µ‡§æ‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§É‡•§
‡§®‡§ø‡§Ç‡§¶‡§Ç‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§µ ‡§∏‡§æ‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§§‡§§‡•ã ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ‡§§‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§®‡•Å ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç‡••‡•©‡•¨‡••

Many unseemly words will be spoken by thine enemies, slandering thy strength; what more painful than that? (36)

‡§Ö‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•ç = ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§æ‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§®‡•ç ‡§µ‡§æ‡§¶‡§æ‡§®‡•ç improper to be spoken, words; ‡§ö and; ‡§¨‡§π‡•Ç‡§®‡•ç many; ‡§µ‡§¶‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç‡§§‡§ø will say; ‡§§‡§µ thy; ‡§Ö‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§æ‡§Énon-friends; ‡§®‡§ø‡§Ç‡§¶‡§Ç‡§§‡§É slandering; ‡§§‡§µ thy; ‡§∏‡§æ‡§Æ‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Çpower; ‡§§‡§§‡§É than that; ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ‡§§‡§∞‡§Ç more painful; ‡§®‡•Å indeed; ‡§ï‡§ø‡§Æ‡•ç what.

‡§π‡§§‡•ã ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§Ç ‡§ú‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§µ‡§æ ‡§≠‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•á ‡§Æ‡§π‡•Ä‡§Æ‡•ç‡•§
‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡•Å‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§† ‡§ï‡•å‡§Ç‡§§‡•á‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡§É‡••‡•©‡•≠‡••

‡§π‡§§‡§É slain; ‡§µ‡§æ or; ‡§™‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø (thou) wilt obtain; ‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ‡§∞‡•ç‡§ó‡§Çheaven; ‡§ú‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æhaving conquered; ‡§µ‡§æor; ‡§≠‡•ã‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•á (thon ) wilt enjoy;‡§Æ‡§π‡•Ä‡§Æ‡•çthe earth; ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç therefore; ‡§â‡§§‡•ç‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§† stand up; ‡§ï‡•å‡§Ç‡§§‡•á‡§Ø O Kaunteya; ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§Ø for battle; ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡§É = ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§É ‡§®‡§ø‡§∂‡•ç‡§ö‡§Ø‡§É ‡§Ø‡•á‡§® ‡§∏‡§É made, decision, by whom, he.

‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ‡•á ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•á ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§≤‡§æ‡§≠‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ‡§≠‡•å ‡§ú‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ú‡§Ø‡§æ‡•§

Taking as equal pleasure and pain, gain and loss, victory and defeat, gird thee for the battle; thus thou shalt not incur sin. (38)

‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ‡•á = ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ñ‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§¶‡•Å‡§É‡§ñ‡§Ç ‡§ö pleasure, and, pain, and; ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•á equal; ‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ having made; ‡§≤‡§æ‡§≠‡§æ‡§≤‡§æ‡§≠‡•å = ‡§≤‡§æ‡§≠‡§É ‡§ö ‡§Ö‡§≤‡§æ‡§≠‡§É ‡§ö gain, and, loss, and; ‡§ú‡§Ø‡§æ‡§ú‡§Ø‡•å = ‡§ú‡§Ø‡§É ‡§ö ‡§Ö‡§ú‡§Ø‡§É ‡§ö, victory, and, defeat, and; ‡§§‡§§‡§É then ; ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§Ø for the battle; ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ join (thou ); ‡§® not; ‡§è‡§µ‡§Ç thus; ‡§™‡§æ‡§™‡§Ç sin; ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§æ‡§™‡•ç‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ‡§∏‡•á (thon) shalt obtain.

‡§è‡§∑‡§æ ‡§§‡•á‡§Ω‡§≠‡§ø‡§π‡§ø‡§§‡§æ ‡§∏‡§æ‡§Ç‡§ñ‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á ‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡•á ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§Æ‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•É‡§£‡•Å‡•§

This teaching set forth to thee is in accordance with the SaÃÇ·πÖkhya; hear it now according to the Yoga, imbued with which teaching, O P√¢rtha, thou shalt cast away the bonds of action. (39)

‚ÄúThe flowery speech that the anwise utter, O PaÃÇrtha, clinging to the word of the Veda, saying there is nothing else, ensouled by selfish desire and longing after heaven‚Äî(the flowery speech) that offereth only rebirth as the (ultimate) fruit of action, and is full of (recommendations to) various rites for the sake of (gaining) enjoyments and sovereignty,‚Äî the thought of those who are misled by that (speech) and cling to pleasures and lordship, not being inspired with resolution, is not engaged in concentration.‚Äù

‡§Ö‡§™‡§π‡•É‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ö‡•á‡§§‡§É ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç dragged away, mind, whose, of them; ‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§∏‡§æ‡§Ø‡§æ‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ determinate (see 41); ‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§É thought; ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§æ‡§ß‡•å in SamaÃÇdhi; ‡§® not; ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ß‡•Ä‡§Ø‡§§‡•á is fixed.

The Vedas deal with the three attributes¬π; be thou above these three attributes, O Arjuna; beyond the pairs of opposites, ever steadfast in [purity11,careless of possessions, full of the SELF. (45)

‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§æ‡§É = ‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§∑‡§Ø‡§É ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•á the three attributes, subject-matter, whose, they; ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§æ‡§É (the) Vedas; ‡§®‡§ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§∞‡•à‡§ó‡•Å‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡§É without the three attributes; ‡§≠‡§µ be; ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§ú‡•Å‡§® O Arjuna; ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§¶‡•ç‡§µ‡§Éwithout the pairs (of opposites ); ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§É = ‡§®‡§ø‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§Ç ‡§∏‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡•á ‡§§‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§†‡§§‡§ø ‡§á‡§§‡§ø constantly, in sattva, (whoj dwells, thus; ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§Æ‡§É = ‡§® ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡§ø ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§É ‡§ö ‡§ï‡•ç‡§∑‡•á‡§Æ‡§É ‡§ö ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§É not, is, earning, and, maintaining, and, whose, he; ‡§Ü‡§§‡•ç‡§Æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç full of the Self.

‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡§∞‡•ç‡§• ‡§â‡§¶‡§™‡§æ‡§®‡•á ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§É ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡•ç‡§≤‡•Å‡§§‡•ã‡§¶‡§ï‡•á‡•§

1 Gu·πáas = attributes, or forms of energy. They are Sattva, rhythm or purity ; Rajas, activity or passion; Tamas, inertia or darkness.

‡§Ø‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç as much; ‡§Ö‡§∞‡•ç‡§•‡§É use; ‡§â‡§¶‡§™‡§æ‡§®‡•á in a small pool; ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡§§‡§É everywhere; ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡•ç‡§≤‡•Å‡§§‡•ã‡§¶‡§ï‡•á = ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§™‡•ç‡§≤‡•Å‡§§‡•á ‡§â‡§¶‡§ï‡•á (in the state of a place & being) flooded, (in, i.e, with) water; ‡§§‡§æ‡§µ‡§æ‡§®‡•ç so much; ‡§∏‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§∑‡•Å (in) all; ‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡•á‡§∑‡•Å in the Vedas; ‡§¨‡•ç‡§∞‡§æ‡§π‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø of the Brahmana; ‡§µ‡§ø‡§ú‡§æ‡§®‡§§‡§É (of) learned.

‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡•ç‡§Ø‡•á‡§µ‡§æ‡§ß‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•á ‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§´‡§≤‡•á‡§∑‡•Å ‡§ï‡§¶‡§æ‡§ö‡§®‡•§
‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§´‡§≤‡§π‡•á‡§§‡•Å‡§∞‡•ç‡§≠‡•Ç‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ ‡§§‡•á ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ó‡•ã‡§Ω‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§ø‡••‡•™‡•≠‡••

Thy business is with the action only, never with its fruits; so let not the fruit of action be thy motive, nor be thou to inaction attached. (47)

‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§ø in the action; ‡§è‡§µ only; ‡§Ö‡§ß‡§ø‡§ï‡§æ‡§∞‡§Éright; ‡§§‡•á thee; ‡§Æ‡§æ not; ‡§´‡§≤‡•á‡§∑‡•Å in the fruits; ‡§ï‡§¶‡§æ‡§ö‡§® at any time; ‡§Æ‡§ænot; ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§´‡§≤‡§π‡•á‡§§‡•Å‡§É = ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§É ‡§´‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§π‡•á‡§§‡•Å‡§É ‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∏‡§É of action, fruit, motive, whose, he; ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§É be;‡§Æ‡§æ not; ‡§§‡•á thy; ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ó‡§É attachment; ‡§Ö‡§∏‡•ç‡§§‡•Å let(there) be; ‡§Ö‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§£‡§ø in inaction.

‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§É ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ó‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§ß‡§®‡§Ç‡§ú‡§Ø‡•§
‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É ‡§∏‡§Æ‡•ã ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó ‡§â‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á‡••‡•™‡•Æ‡••

Perform action, O Dhana√±jaya, dwelling in union with the divine, renouncing attachments, and balanced evenly in success and failure:equilibrium is called yoga. (48)

‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§∏‡•ç‡§•‡§É yoga-seated; ‡§ï‡•Å‡§∞‡•Å, perform; ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§£‡§ø actions; ‡§∏‡§Ç‡§ó‡§Ç attachment; ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ having abandoned; ‡§ß‡§®‡§Ç‡§ú‡§Ø O Dhana√±jaya; ‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§É = ‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•å ‡§ö ‡§Ö‡§∏‡§ø‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•å ‡§ö in success, and, in non-

success, and; ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§É the same; ‡§≠‡•Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æhaving become; ‡§∏‡§Æ‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§Ç equality; ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§É yoga; ‡§â‡§ö‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡•á is called.

‡§¶‡•Ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£ ‡§π‡•ç‡§Ø‡§µ‡§∞‡§Ç ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ ‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§®‡§Ç‡§ú‡§Ø‡•§
‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•å ‡§∂‡§∞‡§£‡§Æ‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ ‡§ï‡•É‡§™‡§£‡§æ‡§É ‡§´‡§≤‡§π‡•á‡§§‡§µ‡§É‡••‡•™‡•Ø‡••

Far lower than the Yoga of Discrimination is action, O Dhana√±jaya. Take thou refuge in the Pure Reason; pitiable are they who work for fruit. (49)

‡§¶‡•Ç‡§∞‡•á‡§£ by far; ‡§π‡§ø indeed; ‡§Ö‡§µ‡§∞‡§Ç inferior; ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ action; ‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç = ‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•á‡§É ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§æ‡§§‡•ç of discrimination, than the yoga; ‡§ß‡§®‡§Ç‡§ú‡§Ø 0 Dhana√±jaya; ‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•åin (the) Pure Reason; ‡§∂‡§∞‡§£‡§Ç refuge; ‡§Ö‡§®‡•ç‡§µ‡§ø‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ, seek; ‡§ï‡•É‡§™‡§£‡§æ‡§É pitiable; ‡§´‡§≤‡§π‡•á‡§§‡§µ‡§É = ‡§´‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§π‡•á‡§§‡•Å‡§É ‡§Ø‡•á‡§∑‡§æ‡§Ç ‡§§‡•á fruit, motive, whose, they.

‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ã ‡§ú‡§π‡§æ‡§§‡•Ä‡§π ‡§â‡§≠‡•á ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•á‡•§
‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§¶‡•ç‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§æ‡§Ø ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§É ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∏‡•Å ‡§ï‡•å‡§∂‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç‡••‡•´‡•¶‡••

United to the Pure Reason, one abandoneth here both good and evil deeds, therefore cleave thou to yoga; yoga is skill in action. ((50)

‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É=‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡•ç‡§Ø‡§æ ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§É with knowledge, endowed; ‡§ú‡§π‡§æ‡§§‡§ø (he) throws away; ‡§á‡§π here; ‡§â‡§≠‡•á both; ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡•á = ‡§∏‡•Å‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ö ‡§¶‡•Å‡§∑‡•ç‡§ï‡•É‡§§‡§Ç ‡§ö good deed, and, evil deed, and; ‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Æ‡§æ‡§§‡•ç therefore; ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§æ‡§Ø for (to) yoga; ‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ú‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§µ be united, (prepare); ‡§Ø‡•ã‡§ó‡§É yoga; ‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§∏‡•Å in actions; ‡§ï‡•å‡§∂‡§≤‡§Æ‡•ç skill.

‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ú ‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ ‡§π‡§ø ‡§´‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§É‡•§
‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¨‡§Ç‡§ß‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§É ‡§™‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§Ç‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§Æ‡•ç‡••‡•´‡•ß‡••

The Sages, united to the Pure Reason, renounce the fruit which action yieldeth, and, liberated from the bonds of birth, they go to the blissful seat. (51)

‡§ï‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡§ú‡§Ç action-born; ‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§Ø‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§É united to Buddhi (see 50); ‡§π‡§ø indeed; ‡§´‡§≤‡§Ç the fruit; ‡§§‡•ç‡§Ø‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡•ç‡§µ‡§æ having abandoned; ‡§Æ‡§®‡•Ä‡§∑‡§ø‡§£‡§É the wise; ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§¨‡§Ç‡§ß‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§É = ‡§ú‡§®‡•ç‡§Æ‡§®‡§É ‡§¨‡§Ç‡§ß‡§æ‡§§‡•ç ‡§µ‡§ø‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§Æ‡•Å‡§ï‡•ç‡§§‡§æ‡§É of birth, from the bondage, freed; ‡§™‡§¶‡§Ç the abode; ‡§ó‡§ö‡•ç‡§õ‡§Ç‡§§‡§øgo; ‡§Ö‡§®‡§æ‡§Æ‡§Ø‡§Ç painless.

‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ ‡§§‡•á ‡§Æ‡•ã‡§π‡§ï‡§≤‡§ø‡§≤‡§Ç ‡§¨‡•Å‡§¶‡•ç‡§ß‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø‡§§‡§∞‡§ø‡§∑‡•ç‡§Ø‡§§‡§ø‡•§
‡§§‡§¶‡§æ ‡§ó‡§Ç‡§§‡§æ‡§∏‡§ø ‡§®‡§ø‡§∞‡•ç‡§µ‡•á‡§¶‡§Ç ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•ã‡§§‡§µ‡•ç‡§Ø‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§∂‡•ç‡§∞‡•Å‡§§‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ö‡••‡•´‡•®‡••

When thy mind shall escape from this tangle of delusion, then thou shalt rise to indifference as to what has been heard and shall be heard. (52)

‡§Ø‡§¶‡§æ when; ‡§§‡•á thy; ‡§Æ‡•ã‡§π‡§ï‡§≤‡§ø‡§≤‡§Ç = ‡§Æ‡•ã‡§π‡§∏‡•ç‡§Ø ‡§ï‡§≤‡§ø‡§≤ of delusion, the confusion
